2025年06月12日更新-【彼得森经济研究所】出口限制如何威胁经济安全-2025.5
25-11 How Export Restrictions Threaten Economic Security
Chad P. Bown
May 2025
ABSTRACT
The fear that a foreign government will impose an export restriction that imperils
another country’s economic and national security has driven part of the recent
turn to industrial policy and the increased use of tariffs. Countries now worry
about disruption not only to their access to energy but also to critical minerals,
semiconductors, medical supplies, and other essential goods. Modern use of
industrial and trade policy is thus often an attempt to move supply chains in the
short term and to sustain them in those new places over the long term, in order
to reduce national vulnerability to disruptions caused by export restrictions.
However, achieving even modest forms of international cooperation on trade and
industrial policy between countries seeking to improve their collective economic
security will also require that these same countries take on new commitments to
discipline their own use of export restrictions toward each other.
JEL codes: F13, L52
Keywords: export restrictions, economic security, industrial policy, tariffs
Note: A revised version of this paper will be published in the Journal of International
Economic Law . This paper is based on the author’s keynote address at the Centre for
Inclusive Trade Policy Academic Conference 2025 in Glasgow. Helpful discussions by
conference participants, Emily Blanchard, Lothar Ehring, Ilaria Espa, Michael Gasiorek,
Bernard Hoekman, Barbara Karni, Marcus Noland, Mona Paulsen, Amy Porges,
Stephanie Rickard, Kristin Vekasi, Alan Winters, and outstanding research assistance by
Christine Wan are gratefully acknowledged. All remaining errors are the author’s.
1750 Massachusetts Avenue, NW | Washington, DC 20036-1903 USA | +1.202.328.9000 | www.piie.com
Chad P. Bown is the
Reginald Jones Senior
Fellow at the Peterson
Institute for International
Economics. He was on
leave for public service as
chief economist for the US
Department of State in the
Biden-Harris administration
from January 2024 until
January 2025. He is
the host of Trade Talks,
a podcast about the
economics of international
trade and policy.
WP 25-11 | MAY 2025 2
1. INTRODUCTION
The world trading system is experiencing a period of upheaval. Unchecked use
of industrial policy and tariffs in the name of economic and national security is
putting strain on international cooperation even among like-minded countries
with common fears about their economic vulnerabilities.
The root of these policies is often the concern that a foreign government
will create disorder by imposing an export restriction. Export restrictions are
one of the least disciplined and most disruptive policies of the trading system.
Yet, restoring some cooperation over industrial policy and tariffs—to address
concerns between like-minded countries over their common, excessive economic
dependencies—will require that governments of these countries also confront
their own use of export restrictions.
Disruptive export restrictions are not new to the postwar global economy.
In 1973, the Arab members of the Organization of the Petroleum Exporting
Countries (OPEC) cartel limited energy production and placed an embargo on
exports to the United States and other countries supporting Israel during the
Arab–Israeli War. OPEC’s restrictions set off a period of stagflation—high inflation
combined with low economic growth—and led to political turmoil in the United
States and other high-income countries.
The fact that disruptions caused by export restrictions can trigger an
outsized counter-response is also not new. The policy reaction to the OPEC
shock, for example, was profound. [1] The United States prioritized energy security
as a key part of its domestic and foreign policy, including decisions about
whether to wage war. Europe made consequential choices as to the form of its
diversification, increasing its reliance on natural gas piped in from the Soviet
Union and then Russia. That decision created myriad transatlantic disputes over
US sanctions and culminated in Russia’s full-scale invasion of Ukraine in 2022.
The oil embargo also led to novel policy initiatives to build stockpiles, including
creation of the US Strategic Petroleum Reserve. It also led to the cooperative
response—involving a small group of OECD countries—to form the International
Energy Agency (IEA) in 1974 and engage in a collective system of rules-based
inventory management and coordinated interventions in response to shortages. [2]
Attempts have also been made to impose additional disciplines on export
restrictions. For example, the effects from the OPEC embargo drove the US
negotiating mandate for the Tokyo Round of the General Agreement on Tariffs
and Trade (GATT), in the Trade Act of 1974, to tackle export restrictions and
charges. [3] Because most of the key OPEC members at the time were not GATT
Contracting Parties, this and other efforts to introduce additional ways to
discipline export restrictions mostly failed. [4] Those failures contributed to today’s
problem of excessive economic dependencies generating security vulnerabilities,
1 See Daniel Yergin, The Prize: The Epic Quest for Oil, Money, and Power (New York: Free Press,
1991).
2 See Craig S. Bamberger, The History of the IEA: The First 30 Years (Paris: International Energy
Agency, 2004).
3 See the “Access to Supplies” of Section 108 of Trade Act of 1974. See also Frieder Roessler,
‘GATT and Access to Supplies,’ 9 Journal of World Trade Law (1975) at 25–40.
4 For a GATT Secretariat review, see GATT, ‘Export Restrictions and Charges. Background Note
by the Secretariat,’ MTN.GNG/NG2/W/40, 8 August 1989.
WP 25-11 | MAY 2025 3
as Biden administration National Security Advisor Jake Sullivan noted in an
April 2023 speech: [5]
Economic integration didn’t stop China from expanding its military ambitions
in the region or stop Russia from invading its democratic neighbors. Neither
country had become more responsible or cooperative. And ignoring economic
dependencies that had built up over the decades of liberalization had become
really perilous—from energy uncertainty in Europe to supply-chain vulnerabilities
in medical equipment, semiconductors, and critical minerals. These were the kinds
of dependencies that could be exploited for economic or geopolitical leverage.
Today, achieving even modest international cooperation between like-minded
countries to discipline the extraordinary and sometimes disorderly use of tariffs,
industrial policy, local (or preferential) content requirements, as well as invocations
of exceptions for national security, will be more likely if their policymakers
simultaneously grapple with the fundamental underlying fear of being cut off from
supplies because of foreign use of export restrictions, even vis-à-vis each other.
The rest of this paper is organized as follows. Section 2 presents a simple
three-country economic model to review how the fear of export restrictions is
driving efforts to establish new sources of supply. It also explores the tradeoffs
associated with different policy instruments deployed to create new supplies and
reestablishes the basic theoretical case for mutual gains to like-minded countries
from the adoption of disciplines to restrain their own use of export restrictions
toward each other. Section 3 presents recent examples of vulnerabilities and
policy actions taken by Europe, the United States (under both the Biden and
Trump administrations), Japan, and other economies. Section 4 describes the
GATT/World Trade Organization (WTO) rules on export restrictions as well as
potential new disciplines emerging elsewhere. Section 5 concludes.
Before moving on, it is important to clarify three points. First, the focus of
this paper is on export restrictions for market dominance purposes, as opposed
to export controls for dual-use goods. The former case refers to goods that, at
least in theory, could be made anywhere, because the technology and production
process are widely available, but are not made in a sufficiently diverse set of
locations, perhaps because of the difficulty of overcoming established economic
forces of agglomeration. In the latter case, it is technologically impossible for
outsiders to produce the good, and the purpose of the export control is to limit
the spread of the technology itself. (Put differently, this paper does not address
the United States’ “small yard, high fence” policy. [6] )
Second, the paper often refers to “export restriction,” noting that such a
policy could be imposed as either a quota (i.e., a volume limit, including a ban) or
a duty (i.e., a tax, including at a prohibitive level). Most of the economic efficiency
and incentive arguments explored below do not require distinguishing between
quotas and duties.
Third, at the time of writing, it was not clear whether the Trump
administration would work with historical partners and allies on some of the
5 White House, ‘Remarks by National Security Advisor Jake Sullivan on Renewing American
Economic Leadership at the Brookings Institution,’ 27 April 2023.
6 White House, ‘Remarks by National Security Advisor Jake Sullivan on Renewing American
Economic Leadership at the Brookings Institution,’ 27 April 2023.
WP 25-11 | MAY 2025 4
common challenges identified below. [7] Any intention to do so will need a
commitment to ending its coercive use of tariffs against these countries in
addition to addressing concerns over future use of export restrictions. [8]
2. A SIMPLE ECONOMIC FRAMEWORK
Consider the following simple model of three countries. Initially, country A is the
only supplier of a critical good, and countries B and C are pure importers. Dozens
of firms in country A operate under conditions of perfect competition. Later
sections consider market structures other than perfect competition, including
one in which geographic concentration of production may arise organically
from agglomeration economies (and the spillovers associated with positive
externalities). [9]
2.1 Noncooperative policy and the benefit to international cooperation over
export restrictions
Next consider policy. If the government of country A does not intervene with
policy to coordinate the behavior of its firms, countries B and C will import goods
from country A at competitive prices. In economic efficiency terms, countries B
and C are unconcerned that country A is the only supplier, because prices are the
same as they would be if the industry were characterized by competitive firms
producing locally.
On the other hand, the government of country A could introduce a policy to
coordinate the joint exports of its firms. [10] In a static setting, country A’s optimal
national policy would be an export tax. [11] An optimal tax would restrict exports,
thereby increasing the price paid by importers in countries B and C. (Country
A would enjoy a terms-of-trade gain, and countries B and C would experience
a terms-of-trade loss.) Relative to no policy intervention, economic well-being
would improve in country A as long as the gain in tax revenue were larger than
the efficiency losses to local producers (too little output) and local consumers
(too much domestic consumption). However, this gain would come at the
expense of larger losses to economic well-being in countries B and C.
The beggar-thy-neighbor nature of the policy outcome creates a potential
efficiency role for international cooperation. Imagine, for example, a triangular
and symmetrical world with a second good, for which B is the monopolist and
A and C are the importers, and a third good, for which C is the monopolist and
A and B the importers. Suppose that each country imposes its noncooperative
7 See Chad P. Bown, ‘Trump’s trade war timeline 2.0: An up-to-date guide,’ PIIE Realtime
Economics, 23 April 2025.
8 See Chad P. Bown and Douglas A. Irwin, ‘The Incoherent Case for Tariffs. Trump’s Fixation on
Economic Coercion Will Subvert His Economic Goals,’ Foreign Affairs, 11 March 2025.
9 See Chad P. Bown, ‘Paul Krugman talks trade, industrial policy, and Trump,’ Trade Talks podcast
episode 206, 16 March 2025.
10 Despite national-level incentives to organize such exporters, some countries, including the
United States, have domestic institutions that prevent this possibility. For example, Article 1,
Section 9 of the US Constitution prohibits Congress from imposing taxes or duties on articles
exported from any state.
11 Alternatively, an economically equivalent policy could be constructed as an export quota
in which the government auctions quota licenses, thereby generating revenue equal to the
revenue generated by the export tax.
WP 25-11 | MAY 2025 5
policy of a nationally optimal export restriction. Starting at such a beggar-thyneighbor outcome, no single country has the incentive to unilaterally lower its
export tax, because doing so would only make it worse off.
A more efficient outcome would be for countries to reciprocally reduce
their export taxes. One role for an international agreement would therefore be
to establish a negotiation process and set of commitments for A, B, and C to
simultaneously reduce and then legally bind their export restrictions. [12] This logic
is central to the economic theory underpinning the GATT and the WTO for the
import-restricting policy instruments introduced by Kyle Bagwell and Robert
Staiger in the 1990s. [13]
It is thus possible, at least in theory, to negotiate disciplines on export
restrictions to coordinate policy changes and address this prisoner’s dilemma
problem. A framework for how to do so could be adapted from the reciprocal
tariff negotiations and Article II bindings that the GATT adopted for imports
beginning in 1947 and deployed successfully for more than half a century and
which a substantial line of economic theory and empirical evidence has come to
understand. [14] (I discuss the legal elements in Section 4.)
2.2 The changed world and governments’ desire to rework supply chains
Consider again the setting of only one good, in which country A is the
monopolist supplier and countries B and C are importers. Now assume that there
is a shock to the system, such as a breakdown in cooperation between country
A and countries B and C. A could become autocratic, for example, and B and
C could discover that country A has foreign policy ambitions that will affect
its commercial policy choices in ways that are no longer purely economic (by
banning exports, for example). [15]
Therefore, define B and C as “like-minded” countries because they both seek
economic security in the form of a surety of supply from a source that is not
solely in country A. In this case, countries B and C might use a combination of
stockpiling, industrial policy (subsidies), and discriminatory tariffs on imports
from A to incentivize new sources of supply to arise outside of A. Consider the
following economic tradeoffs under different policy scenarios in this setting:
Countries B and C stockpile supplies outside of A. Many goods are storable.
If there is a way for B and C to establish a sufficiently sizable supply outside
of A, they can threaten to add some of those supplies to markets if country A
suddenly cuts off its exports. An inventory management system can help deter
country A from restricting exports for market power purposes in the first place.
12 In the absence of symmetry, to generate gains to economic well-being for all sides, the
reduction of export restrictions could be tied to something else, such as a reduction in an
import restriction for a different good or enforcement of foreign intellectual property rights.
These possibilities lie outside the modeling framework considered here.
13 See, for example, Kyle Bagwell and Robert W. Staiger, ‘An Economic Theory of GATT,’ 89
American Economic Review (1989) at 215–248 and Kyle Bagwell and Robert W. Staiger The
Economics of the World Trading System (Cambridge, MA: The MIT Press, 2002).
14 See Robert W. Staiger, A World Trading System for the Twenty-First Century (Cambridge, MA:
MIT Press, 2022).
15 Alternative unmodeled shocks could have similar effects. One would be if country A suddenly
developed a new institutional framework to coordinate export restrictions among its perfectly
competitive firms.
WP 25-11 | MAY 2025 6
Stockpiling is costly, however, especially across countries. [16] It is also infeasible
for products that are perishable or quickly become obsolete in the face of rapid
technological change. In addition, once stockpiles exist, it may be difficult for
democratically elected policymakers to follow rules instead of using discretion.
For example, governments may feel pressure to release supplies for political
reasons—to lower prices and influence voters—in the lead-up to an election.
Countries B and/or C offer subsidies for industries to exit country A. Consider a
subsidy that is contingent only on firms leaving the country of concern (A), not
on the location of the new production facilities (i.e., they could be located in B
and/or C). Such a subsidy is the most efficient policy, because it introduces the
fewest additional distortions.
The subsidy establishes an incentive for new sources of supply to arise
outside of A. It allows firms—not the government—to choose the secondbest production location. (This feature is important if governments lack
information available to firms about locational cost advantages, the benefits of
agglomeration economies, and other factors.)
Countries B and/or C offer subsidies contingent on production taking place in
their country. A country-specific subsidy creates an incentive for new sources
of supply to arise outside of A, but relative to a subsidy that is not contingent
on location, it introduces potential distortions. If, for example, B is a more costly
supplier than C, offering the subsidy only if production takes place in B leads
to an inefficient economic outcome. Alternatively, if there are economies of
scale, the economically efficient outcome would be for only one country to
produce, rather than for each country to produce because each country offered a
subsidy to do so.
To further complicate matters, there may be noneconomic benefits to
local production, especially in the case of goods that are essential for security
purposes. One benefit is policy control. It is easier for policymakers to create
incentives to quickly ramp up production in a domestic industry than to do so in
an industry located abroad. [17] Producing domestically also makes it possible to
hoard supplies in an emergency.
Thus, the presence of incentives that sometimes pit B against C provides one
motivation for them to consider ways to discipline their use of export restrictions
toward each other at the same time that they are working through their tariff and
subsidy choices regarding how to move supply chains away from country A (as
further discussed in Section 2.3 below).
Countries B and C jointly impose discriminatory tariffs on imports from
country A. A tariff affects two types of economic incentives: It stimulates
additional production (like a production subsidy), and it disincentivizes
domestic consumption (like a consumption tax). Tariffs would therefore
16 See Albert L. Nichols and Richard J. Zeckhauser, ‘Stockpiling Strategies and Cartel Prices,’ 8
Bell Journal of Economics (1977) at 66–96.
17 For a related discussion that arose in the context of establishing COVID-19 vaccine supply
chains, see Chad P. Bown, ‘The WTO and vaccine supply chain resilience during a pandemic,’
26 Journal of International Economic Law (2023) at 343–362 as well as Chad P. Bown,
Christopher M. Snyder, and Robert W. Staiger, ‘Vaccine supply chains and international
cooperation,’ (Hannover, NH: Dartmouth College manuscript, 2022).
WP 25-11 | MAY 2025 7
establish an incentive for new sources of supply to arise outside of A but at
the potential added cost of raising prices to consumers in B and C if the tariffs
stimulate relatively inefficient production, as in the contingent subsidy case
described above.
However, if the country B and C tariffs are neutral, an unacknowledged
potential benefit of them (relative to a location-contingent subsidy) is that
the tariff policy allows firms—not governments—to choose their second-best
production location, as in the case of the country A-exit subsidy.
2.3 Why countries B and C need to simultaneously consider new disciplines
for their export restrictions, in addition to coordination of trade and
industrial policy
Suppose countries B and C plan to use some combination of industrial and
trade policy to create a second source of supply outside of country A. For
concreteness, assume that production will end up located in country B. All else
equal, country C would find several problems with this new outcome, which
would affect its willingness to participate in the supply diversification plan in
the first place.
First, country C would still find itself subject to potential export restrictions
(now from B instead of A), because of hoarding (during emergencies) or a new
market dominance problem that can result in optimal export taxes. Second, C’s
exposure to geographically concentrated shocks would not improve by much,
because the only change is to single source from B instead of A. From country
C’s perspective, all that has happened is it replaced its “country A problem” with
a new “country B problem.”
What would it take to ensure that both country B and country C were content
with the new outcome in which the additional source of supply were from
country B? Country C would want to maintain some minimal access to supply
from country A, in order to ensure that there is a competitor to the new industry
emerging in country B and as a diversification benefit in the face of geographicspecific shocks to B. To achieve the right balance, country B and country C
would thus first need to tweak their trade and industrial policies to jointly
manage A’s export access to their combined market. (If there is too much access,
competitors in B may not emerge; if there is too little access, country C will not
participate in the plan.)
The second and final element is that B would also need to agree to discipline
its own use of export restrictions against C in the future. Such a commitment is
essential to incentivize C to participate and to make the overall plan work.
2.4 How country A’s policy reactions can weaken the incentives offered by
countries B and C
Before moving on, it is worth considering one added complexity to this already
complicated setting. What happens when this is no longer a one shot game,
and country A can affect market outcomes through its own response to the
policies of B and C?
Much like parallels to the behavior of a monopolist in such a setting, the
government of A might want its firms to temporarily expand output to reduce
prices to deter entry by firms in B and/or C, despite their governments’ new
WP 25-11 | MAY 2025 8
policies. In order to maintain that monopolist position over the long run, the
government of A may thus strategically “switch” its export policy from a
restriction to a subsidy—even if doing so inflicts short-run costs—in order to
maintain the long-run benefits from market dominance.
The effect of the export subsidy would be to expand country A’s output
and exports, lowering the world price and offsetting some of the effect of the
policies by countries B and C to incentivize supply outside of A. Put differently,
sometimes country A can use it market power to restrict exports to the detriment
of countries B and C. Other times, country A can increase exports to deter entry
into the market by firms in B and C, such as when its long-run position of market
dominance is under threat.
3. FEARS OVER EXPORT RESTRICTIONS
Export restrictions (or the credible threat of them) are driving new and initially
uncoordinated sectoral initiatives involving trade and industrial policy. These
efforts can lead to conflicting incentives even between like-minded countries
that face the same underlying concerns. This section highlights examples
and identifies real-world challenges countries have faced as they attempt to
incentivize sourcing outside of countries of concern.
3.1 Russia’s energy export restrictions
Since the 1970s, European countries have sought to diversify their energy
sourcing away from dependence on oil from the Middle East. Part of that strategy
involved turning to the Soviet Union initially (and then Russia after the Soviet
breakup in the 1990s) for imports of natural gas. This natural gas ultimately
reached Europe through politically controversial and economically costly
pipelines that were built despite the objections of the United States. [18]
The United States argued that the pipelines would put broader European
security—and thus its own security, as they were allies under NATO—at risk by
making Europe excessively dependent on Russian gas and unable to take bold
action in the face of conflict. Europe’s first counterargument was that buying
Russian energy would lead to beneficial economic interdependence with its
neighbor, which would moderate Russian policy behavior. Its second argument
was that the United States was being disingenuous and proposing actions aimed
at keeping Europe at a competitive disadvantage, as America’s energy-intensive
industries had access to lower priced inputs due to cheap domestic oil in the
1980s and natural gas after the fracking boom of the 2000s.
Both the United States and Europe turned out to be right. In February
2022, Russia launched a full-scale invasion of Ukraine, weaponizing its supply of
natural gas to Europe. The reduction in gas exports generated political instability
across the continent and contributed to the tepid European policy response to
Russian aggression.
18 For details, see Agathe Demarais, Backfire: How Sanctions Reshape the World Against US
Interests (New York: Columbia University Press, 2022) who describes the governmental
positions and policies regarding the pipelines and the US sanctions attempting to impede their
construction. See also Chad P. Bown, ‘Why sanctions to stop Russian pipelines backfired,’ Trade
Talks podcast episode 178, 19 February 2023.
WP 25-11 | MAY 2025 9
But Europe was right about the effect of cheap Russian gas. Once it was
cut off, energy prices spiked, contributing to an increase in European inflation,
especially in 2022. [19] The German economy stagnated. [20] The Russian invasion
combined with developments in China created a German existential crisis
regarding its lost economic growth model. German industry no longer had access
to cheap inputs (Russian energy), and it was steadily losing access to the key
export market (China) that had driven its export growth over the prior 30 years.
Europe was not helped by the fact that, independently, the United States
was passing the Inflation Reduction Act, which allocated billions of dollars of
subsidies for local clean energy projects. Under its 2020 European Green Deal
legislation, relative energy prices in Europe were trending upward through
policy efforts to tax carbon emissions, via the tightening of its Emissions Trading
System, to reduce the allocation of free permits, and to implement a carbon
border adjustment mechanism. [21] As stressed in the subsequent Draghi Report,
Europe was becoming increasingly worried about its industrial competitiveness. [22]
In the face of its lost access to Russian energy supplies, Europe’s short-run
policy response was to scramble to find new sources. The United States quickly
redirected exports of liquified natural gas (LNG) to the continent, prioritizing
them over exports to China that had expanded to satisfy the purchase
commitment requirements of the US-China “Phase One Agreement” of 2020. [23]
To replace the gas that had come from Russia through pipelines, Germany, the
Netherlands, and other European countries quickly built new LNG regasification
facilities to receive gas shipments. [24] These efforts were part of the European
Commission’s hastily arranged RePowerEU Plan, announced in May 2022, to
end reliance on Russian energy by 2030. [25] The Plan also included efforts to
conserve energy and continue Europe’s clean energy transition under the
European Green Deal.
Finally, the relevance of export restrictions affecting even like-minded
countries arose during this period in at least two instances. In late 2022, Europe
likely worried as the Biden administration had to fight off intense domestic

2024).
20 German Council of Economic Experts, Annual Report 2024/25 . 13 November 2024.

and Adam Posen (eds.), The Green Frontier: Assessing the Economic Implications of Climate
Action (Washington, DC: Peterson Institute for International Economics, 2024, 331–371).

(Brussels: European Commission, September 2024).

Agreement between the Government of the United States of America and the Government of
the People’s Republic Of China’, available at https://ustr.gov/countries-regions/china-mongolia-
taiwan/peoples-republic-china/phase-one-trade-agreement/text, last accessed April 30,
2025. For European imports of natural gas, see Ugné Keliauskaitė, Ben McWilliams, Giovanni
Sgaravatti, and Georg Zachmann, ‘European natural gas imports,’ Bruegel Datasets, last

24 See Institute for Energy Economics and Financial Analysis, ‘European LNG Tracker,’ Last

WP 25-11 | MAY 2025 10
political pressure not to impose export restrictions on refined oil products
because of low inventories and high inflation heading into the midterm
elections. [26] Similarly, in 2024, the Biden administration put a temporary pause on
future projects to export LNG until after the November election. [27] Although this
decision did not affect contemporaneous sales, it did reinforce worries over the
lack of disciplines over export restrictions. The issue is unlikely to go away, given
the Trump administration’s efforts to establish “energy dominance” and increase
US energy exports, especially of LNG, which could shift European dependence
toward the United States. [28]
It is too early to assess European progress toward energy security over the
long run, but the political importance of the fallout from Russia’s actions cannot
be overstated. The harsh revelation of European vulnerabilities refreshed fears
of stagflation associated with exposure to export restrictions—including across
the Atlantic—that may have faded from memory in the period since the 1970’s
OPEC experience.
3.2 Chinese critical minerals and rare earths
Critical minerals and rare earths are essential to the clean energy transition
(battery electric vehicles, wind turbines); electronics (cell phones, computer
displays); military applications (lasers, satellites); and more. [29] According to the
US Geological Survey, China is among the top producers of 6 of the 10 most
production-concentrated materials, including lithium, graphite, magnesium, and
rare earth metals. [30] These data understate China’s dominance, because they
do not fully capture the concentration of processing of these minerals or the
ownership structure of firms mining or operating in third countries, two areas in
which China plays a large role. [31]
Stéphane Séjourné, the European Commission's Executive Vice-President
for Prosperity and Industrial Strategy, summarized these fears in March 2025
as follows: “We do not want to replace our dependence on fossil fuels with
dependence on raw materials. Chinese lithium will not be tomorrow’s Russian
gas.” [32] In the United States, the Trump administration initiated an investigation
26 Timothy Gardner, ‘U.S. energy secretary urges refiners not to increase fuel exports,’ Reuters, 29
August 2022.
27 Timothy Gardner, ‘Biden pauses LNG export approvals after pressure from climate activists,’
Reuters, 26 January 2024.
28 See White House, ‘Establishing the National Energy Dominance Council,’ Executive Order,
90 FR 9945, 20 February 2025; White House, ‘Unleashing American Energy,’ Executive Order,
90 FR 8353, 29 January 2025.
29 See Brandon S. Tracy, ‘An Overview of Rare Earth Elements and Related Issues for Congress,’
Congressional Research Service, CRS Report for Congress R46618, 24 November 2020.
30 See Figure 3.5 of Przemyslaw Kowalski and Clarisse Legendre, ‘Raw Materials Critical for the
Green Transition: Production, International Trade and Export Restrictions,’ (Paris: OECD Trade
Policy Paper no. 269, April 2023).
31 See the analysis in Luc Leruth, Adnan Mazarei, Pierre Régibeau, and Luc Renneboog, ‘Green
energy depends on critical minerals. Who controls the supply chains?’ (Washington, DC: PIIE
Working Papers 22-12, August 2022).
32 See Robert Hodgson, ‘Chinese lithium must not become ‘the new Russian gas,’ says EU
industry chief,’ Euronews, 25 March 2025.
WP 25-11 | MAY 2025 11
into whether imports of critical minerals pose a threat to national security under
Section 232 of the Trade Expansion Act of 1962, and it signed a reconstruction
investment fund agreement with Ukraine that included critical minerals. [33]
Such fears emerged even before China’s high-profile export restrictions on
rare earth metals in 2010. [34] Those restrictions followed a diplomatic breakdown
between China and Japan after the Japanese Coast Guard and a Chinese fishing
trawler collided near the Senkaku Islands, a territory adjacent to Taiwan that is
administered by Japan but claimed by China. The counter-response to the export
restrictions was formal WTO litigation by Japan, the United States, and the
European Union, which China lost, albeit not until the Appellate Body decision
was released in 2014. [35] The start of that litigation was delayed because the
United States and the European Union were awaiting resolution of a WTO dispute
against China they had brought in 2009 over earlier Chinese export restrictions
on raw materials. (China lost that dispute as well. [36] )
These WTO rulings did little to stop or seemingly slow China’s active use
of export-restricting policies. In 2016, the Obama administration filed a third
challenge to China’s export restrictions for critical minerals. It also initiated
a separate dispute contesting China’s subsidies for aluminum, which likely
would have also included claims of Beijing’s export restrictions on primary
aluminum. [37] (WTO litigation for those disputes never materialized, as the
Trump administration deprioritized the WTO as an approach to resolving
conflicts with China.)
More recently, following escalating trade tensions with the United States
under both the Biden and Trump administrations, China announced a series of
new export restrictions for critical minerals for reasons it now ties to national
security. These included new Chinese export restrictions on germanium, gallium,
graphite, and rare earth processing technology in 2023; on antimony and
tungsten in 2024; and on tellurium, bismuth, indium, molybdenum, samarium,
33 See White House, ‘Ensuring National Security and Economic Resilience Through Section 232
Actions on Processed Critical Minerals and Derivative Products,’ Executive Order, 90 FR 16437,
18 April 2025; US Department of Treasury, ‘Treasury Announces Agreement to Establish United
States-Ukraine Reconstruction Investment Fund’, Press Release, 30 April 2025.
34 See Ilaria Espa, Export Restrictions on Critical Minerals and Metals. Testing the Adequacy of
WTO Disciplines (Cambridge, UK: Cambridge University Press, 2015).
35 China – Measures Related to the Exportation of Rare Earths, Tungsten, and Molybdenum . AB

36 China – Measures Related to the Exportation of Various Raw Materials, AB-2011-5, Reports of

- Export Duties on Certain Raw Materials . Request for Consultations by the United States, WT/

WP 25-11 | MAY 2025 12
gadolinium, terbium, dysprosium, lutetium, scandium, and yttrium in early 2025,
in response to the second Trump administration’s tariff escalation. [38]
Major importing countries have begun to undertake trade and industrial
policy efforts to blunt China’s growing market dominance. Because of
its 2010 experience, the Japanese government was among the first to
intervene, supporting projects in countries like Malaysia and Australia to
incentivize Japanese companies to find alternative sources of supply for rare
earth minerals. [39]
Other major economies got involved much later. In the United States, for
example, the electric vehicle tax credit provisions (Section 30D) of the Inflation
Reduction Act of 2022 grants the consumer subsidy only to electrical vehicle
companies that source from battery manufacturers that are shifting their critical
minerals suppliers away from China. [40] The United States also included some
critical minerals on its list of products subject to the tariffs it imposed on imports
from China under Section 301 of the Trade Act of 1974. [41] In 2023, the European
Commission adopted the Critical Raw Materials Act, which included the explicit
objective of diversifying the European Union’s imports of such materials to
reduce strategic dependencies. [42] The European Union established benchmarks
for 2030 that require that no more than 65 percent of consumption of each
strategic raw material at any relevant stage of processing come from a single
third country.

Metals,’ (Cambridge, MA: Harvard Program on US-Japan Relations Occasional Paper Series 22VK, 2022).
40 See Chad P. Bown, ‘Industrial policy for electric vehicle supply chains and the U.S.-EU fight

(Brussels: Bruegel, 2023, 210–255).
41 See, for example, US Trade Representative, ‘USTR Increases Tariffs Under Section 301 on

42 See ‘European Critical Raw Materials Act. Proposal for a regulation of the European Parliament

WP 25-11 | MAY 2025 13
Eventually, these like-minded countries began to engage cooperatively to
turn their unilateral approaches into plurilateral efforts to attempt to coordinate
their activities. In 2022, the United States, Japan, the European Commission, and
others formed the Minerals Security Partnership (MSP), initially a network of likeminded buyer countries seeking access to critical minerals from outside China. [43]
Eventually, a number of mostly emerging economies with mineral supplies—also
concerned about Chinese market dominance in minerals processing—joined a
companion organization, the MSP Forum. [44] Potential economic benefits for these
countries included a more diverse set of buyers. In September 2024, countries
established the MSP Finance Network, through which their development finance
institutions and export credit agencies came together to better facilitate the
public-private partnerships needed to support projects in third countries. [45]
Since 2010, global demand for critical minerals has risen, in part because of
government policy incentives to accelerate the clean energy transition. Yet, there
has also been significant volatility in global markets and periods of extremely
low prices for some of these critical minerals. Such low prices can work against
initiatives seeking to create and sustain these alternative sources of supply,
like the MSP, as the MSP requires profit-maximizing firms to enter and remain
in the market.
For some critical minerals, China may have been abusing its market power
in the other direction (as proposed in Section 2.4) by periodically expanding
supplies to discourage the new entrants that collaborative efforts like the MSP
might incentivize. Yet, the exact cause of this oversupply was difficult to pin
down. The global downturn in nickel prices in 2023–24, for example, seemed
driven by excess capacity in Indonesia, which had become dominated by Chinese
firms that had invested in the country following Indonesian export restrictions
on nickel ore imposed beginning in 2014. [46] Was the increase in output despite
falling global prices simply endemic to nickel, a commodity prone to boombust cycles? Or was it that Chinese firms in Indonesia were more accepting of
loss-making behavior relative to Western-oriented firms, having grown up in a
different economic system? Or, more nefariously, were Chinese firms in Indonesia
working to keep global prices low under the direction of policymakers in Beijing?
Whatever the mechanism, this excess supply of nickel and low world prices
contributed to firms’ decision to shutter mines and mineral-processing facilities in
critical, non-Chinese supplier countries, including Australia. [47]
These sorts of closures raise difficult, but important, questions for
international cooperation. What if the economic security risk felt by the United
States, Japan, South Korea, or Europe—because of their battery electric vehicle
industries’ need, for example, for processed nickel—means that they have a
stronger desire to keep an Australian mine open than does the government of

44 European Commission, ‘EU and international partners agree to expand cooperation on critical
raw materials,’ Press release, 4 April 2024.
45 US Department of State, ‘Joint Statement on Establishment of the Minerals Security
Partnership Finance Network,’ Press Release, 23 September 2024.
46 See Robert Walker and Hilman Palaon, ‘The future of Indonesia’s green industrial policy’
(Sydney: Lowy Institute, 4 March 2025).
47 BHP, ‘Western Australia Nickel to temporarily suspend operations,’ Press release, 11 July 2024.
WP 25-11 | MAY 2025 14
Australia itself? A slight divergence of interests can make it difficult for even likeminded countries to agree on, let alone coordinate, industrial policy.
When it comes to economic or national security, such an example can also
motivate why a country would subsidize to produce locally in the first place, even
though this might be less efficient than production in a like-minded partner from
which it would hope to eventually obtain the good through international trade.
In the United States, for example, use of the Defense Production Act of 1950
during the recent COVID-19 pandemic showed policymakers how the existence
of domestic facilities can allow policy to induce quicker and larger production
responses in the face of an emergency. [48] For critical minerals, having local mines
or processing facilities can give a government more control over production,
because it is within its own legal jurisdiction and policy reach to increase it.
Next, the IEA is exploring the feasibility of creating stockpiles of certain
critical minerals. In February 2024, it launched the Critical Minerals Security
Programme, which would include “collaborative approaches to security
mechanisms, including voluntary stockpiling.” [49] The United States began
pushing for this effort at least as early as February 2022, when it indicated that
“additional efforts are underway to explore links between the stockpile and
growing critical minerals initiatives under the International Energy Agency”. [50]
The Japanese government was also an early mover, having announced a plan
to stockpile critical minerals as part of it new international resource strategy in
2020. [51] This program might one day work like the IEA’s oil program described
earlier, in which “IEA member countries are required to ensure oil stock levels
equivalent to no less than 90 days of net imports and to be ready to collectively
respond to severe supply disruptions affecting the global oil market.” [52]
3.3 Semiconductors
Semiconductors are another essential input over which policymakers have
emerging fears of disruptions by export restrictions. [53] These worries have been
motivated by two interrelated concerns, one at each end of sophistication of
the chip market.
In the advanced node segment of the market, the first worry concerns
Taiwan—specifically, the Taiwan Semiconductor Manufacturing Company
(TSMC)—which produces more than 90 percent of the world’s high-end
semiconductors, including those needed for advanced weapons systems and
artificial intelligence. This level of geographic concentration is risky for an island
that is vulnerable to wildfires, earthquakes, and typhoons, as well as possible
48 See, for example, Chad P. Bown, ‘COVID-19 vaccine supply chains and the Defense Production
Act,’ 38 Oxford Review of Economic Policy (2022) at 771–796.
49 See IEA, ‘Communique of the 287th Meeting (2024) of the IEA Governing Board at Ministerial
Level,’ (Paris: International Energy Agency, 14 February 2024).
50 See US Department of Energy, ‘U.S. Departments of Energy, State and Defense to Launch
Effort to Enhance National Defense Stockpile with Critical Minerals for Clean Energy
Technologies,’ Press release, 25 February 2022.
51 See IEA, ‘International Resource Strategy - National stockpiling system,’ (Paris: International
Energy Agency, 26 October 2023).
52 See IEA, ‘Oil Stocks of IEA Countries,’ (Paris: International Energy Agency, 13 February 2025).
53 For an in depth discussion, see Chad P. Bown and Dan Wang, ‘Semiconductors and Modern
Industrial Policy,’ 38 Journal of Economic Perspectives (2024) at 81–110.
WP 25-11 | MAY 2025 15
takeover by China. [54] If China did take Taiwan, it could impose export restrictions
and cut the world off from high-end chips. Fear of this scenario motivated
industrial policy arising elsewhere, including the CHIPS and Science Act in
the United States in 2022 and similar government interventions by Japan and
Germany to subsidize the creation of TSMC plants outside Taiwan.
A second concern is that China is on a path to dominating the market for
low-end semiconductors, through a combination of competitiveness, scale, and
government intervention. [55] The fear is that, at some point, China could exploit
its market dominance for this segment of the market. In 2021, a global shortage
of such legacy chips arose for other reasons and was extremely problematic for
companies seeking to manufacture refrigerators, toasters, washing machines,
automobiles, and more. Indeed, the shortage of chips forced the politically
powerful and economically consequential auto sectors in the United States and
Europe to temporarily idle plants and furlough workers.
The United States has incentivized production of legacy node semiconductors
outside of China through two instruments. First, both the first Trump and Biden
administrations increased tariffs on semiconductors from China under Section
This action should create incentives for production in the United States or
third countries, albeit at the potential cost of higher prices to users. Second,
the CHIPS Act included some funding for facilities that produce legacy nodes.
Nevertheless, some government intervention in this segment of the market may
need to be long-lasting to sustain diversification and ensure competition with
China over the long term. (In April 2025, the Trump administration initiated an
investigation into semiconductors under Section 232. [56] )
3.4 Medical supply chains
The COVID-19 pandemic brought to policymakers’ attention fears of
geographically concentrated production of various medical supply chains.
This section focuses on two: personal protective equipment (PPE) and active
pharmaceutical ingredients.
The first serious product shortage during the pandemic was for PPE. [57] The
shortages in Europe were so severe that in March 2020 EU member states
imposed export restrictions on the free flow of PPE between each other. The
European Commission ultimately had to step in to reopen trade between member
states, which it was able to do only by agreeing to temporarily impose export
controls on PPE leaving the European Union.
Similar PPE shortages led the United States to restrict exports in April
These restrictions initially threatened sales from the limited production at
3M plants in the United States to hospitals in Canada and Mexico. After reminders
54 See Yew Lun Tian and Ben Blanchard, ‘China will never renounce right to use force over Taiwan,
Xi says,’ Reuters, 16 October 2022.
55 On China’s historical use of subsidies in the sector, see OECD, ‘Measuring Distortions in
International Markets: The Semiconductor Value Chain,’ (Paris: OECD Trade Policy Paper 234,
2019).
56 Department of Commerce, ‘Notice of Request for Public Comments on Section 232 National
Security Investigation of Imports of Semiconductors and Semiconductor Manufacturing
Equipment,’ 90 FR 15950, 16 April 2025.
57 See Chad P. Bown, ‘How COVID-19 medical supply shortages led to extraordinary trade and
industrial policy,’ 17 Asian Economic Policy Review (2022) at 114–135.
WP 25-11 | MAY 2025 16
that Canada could cut the United States off from key inputs needed to make PPE,
such as medical-grade pulp, US exports to Canada and Mexico were allowed. [58]
Part of the underlying challenge was that the dominant world supplier and
exporter of PPE was China, the country hit first by the pandemic, where a surge
in local demand took export supplies off the world market just as demand in the
rest of the world started to explode. On multiple occasions, outsiders worried
that China might be weaponizing its use of export restrictions on PPE. In March
2020, Beijing denied that it was imposing export restrictions. [59] China was forced
to implement some restrictions in April 2020—not, apparently to weaponize PPE
but to control the quality of its PPE exports, as substandard products had hit the
market, threatening the reputation of the high-quality Chinese PPE industry. [60]
These events ultimately led countries to implement novel forms of industrial
policy, tariffs, or both. In 2020, Japan introduced “China-exit subsidies,”
incentivizing subsidiaries of Japanese firms to set up production facilities outside
China, even if the next-best option was not in Japan. [61] The fear of being cut off by
the United States led Canada to subsidize a 3M plant in Ontario. [62] Its worries over
being held up by China led the United States to spend over $1 billion in 2020–21 to build out a more fulsome domestic supply chain for PPE. To help sustain it in the face of falling prices for PPE when demand eventually normalized, in 2024, the Biden administration increased tariffs on PPE imports from China
One lesson from this experience is that a more efficient risk-sharing policy
for countries with integrated supply networks—like Canada and the United
States—would involve coordinated subsidies for PPE manufacturing and
inventory management policies as well as tighter disciplines constraining the
use of export restrictions toward each other. The United States-Mexico-Canada
Agreement (USMCA) does not include specific additional rules in this area, but
the agreement is up for review in 2026, when they could be added.
Even as the COVID-19 pandemic subsided, policymakers identified new
concerns over medical supply chain vulnerabilities. One involves persistent
shortages of generic drugs. [64] Although these shortage have not yet been
linked to weaponized export restrictions, Europe, the United States, and many
other countries have commissioned studies to examine their dependencies
58 The Canadian Press, ‘B.C. mill doubles production, ships medical pulp south despite U.S. export
ban,’ 5 April 2020.
59 See Wang Hui, ‘China denies banning export of face masks,’ CGTN, 5 March 2020.
60 See Kate O’Keeffe, Liza Lin, and Eva Xiao, ‘China’s export restrictions strand medical goods
US needs to fight coronavirus, State Department says,’ Wall Street Journal, 16 April 2020 and
Chad P. Bown, ‘COVID-19: Demand spikes, export restrictions, and quality concerns imperil
poor country access to medical supplies,’ in Richard E. Baldwin and Simon J. Evenett (eds.),
COVID-19 and Trade Policy: Why Turning Inward Won’t Work (London, UK: CEPR Press, 2020,
31–47).
61 See Nikkei Asia, ‘Japan Reveals 87 Projects Eligible for ‘China Exit’ Subsidies,’ 17 July 2020.
62 Ontario, ‘Ontario partners with Federal Government and 3M Canada on new N-95 respirator
manufacturing facility,’ News release, Office of the Premier, 21 August 2020.
63 Office of the US Trade Representative, ‘Notice of Modification: China’s Acts, Policies and
Practices Related to Technology Transfer, Intellectual Property and Innovation,’ 89 FR 76581, 18
September 2024.
64 Anaïs Galdin, ‘Resilience of Global Supply Chains and Generic Drug Shortages,’ (Hanover, NH:
Dartmouth College, manuscript, January 2025).
WP 25-11 | MAY 2025 17
on China, especially for the supply of active pharmaceutical ingredients and
key starting materials, which are the building blocks for generics and other
essential medicines. [65]
While there are many interrelated reasons why such a dependency may
be emerging, the United States, the European Union, and others have not
yet used trade and industrial policy to the same extent in these markets as
in other sectors, either independently or cooperatively. (In April 2025, the
Trump administration initiated an investigation under Section 232 to determine
the effects on national security of imports of pharmaceutical ingredients. [66] )
If they do, it would also be useful to simultaneously work out disciplines for
export restrictions.
4. DISCIPLINES IN THE GATT/WTO AND ELSEWHERE
This section introduces some of the existing and emerging disciplines for export
restrictions in international agreements. [67]
4.1 Disciplines under the multilateral trading system
The drafters of the GATT included some disciplines on the use of export
restrictions in the 1947 agreement. Article XI prohibits the use of quantitative
restrictions on the export side, equivalent to its ban on quotas and volume limits
on the import side.
The two types of restrictions differ in several ways, however. First, the ban
on export restrictions does not apply to “export prohibitions or restrictions
temporarily applied to prevent or relieve critical shortages of foodstuffs or
other products essential to the exporting contracting party” (Article XI 2(a)).
This provision creates a potentially large exemption, as “essential” products
are not defined.
Second, with limited exceptions, the substitutable policy of an export duty
is not disciplined under the GATT. [68] This stance stands in sharp contrast to
import duties, which have been negotiated reciprocally over time (under Article
XXVIII: bis), are bound legally under Article II, and for which there are numerous
well-defined exceptions in case of emergency safeguards (Article XIX) or other
65 See White House, ‘ Building Resilient Supply Chains, Revitalizing American Manufacturing,
and Fostering Broad-Based Growth. 100-Day Reviews under Executive Order 14017,’ June
2021; White House, ‘ 2021-2024 Quadrennial Supply Chain Review,’ December 2024; European
Commission, ‘ Strategic Dependencies and Capabilities’ (Brussels: Commission Staff Working
Document SWD(2021) 352 final, 5 May 2021); and OECD, Securing Medical Supply Chains in a
Post-Pandemic World (Paris: OECD Health Policy Studies, February 2024).
66 Department of Commerce, ‘Notice of Request for Public Comments on Section 232 National
Security Investigation of Imports of Pharmaceuticals and Pharmaceutical Ingredients, 90 FR
15951, 16 April 2025.
67 For an historical account of broader economic security concerns in the trading system, see
Mona Pinchis-Paulsen, ‘The Past, Present, and Potential of Economic Security,’ Yale Journal of
International Law, forthcoming.
68 One historical exception is that Malaysia and Singapore made commitments not to impose
export restrictions for tin plate, conditional on the United States not subsidizing domestic tin
plate (see Roessler 1975, p. 35). A second is that China took on legally binding commitments
for its export taxes under its 2001 WTO Protocol of Accession; yet, as described above, at best
this has had a limited impact on China’s use of export restrictions in practice.
WP 25-11 | MAY 2025 18
“unfair” behaviors, such as dumped or subsidized imports that cause injury to a
domestic import-competing industry (Article VI).
During discussions in the 1970s over new disciplines for export restrictions,
Frieder Roessler (1975, p. 37) argued that if countries want to do more, “the
binding of export tariffs requires, just as the binding of import duties, a
carefully balanced framework to ensure, on the one hand, that the concession
is not invalidated by other governmental measures and, on the other, that the
concession can be withdrawn in emergency situations.”
4.2 Disciplines emerging elsewhere
Other economic integration agreements include stricter language seeking to
limit the use of export restrictions. [69] Perhaps the deepest such agreement, the
Treaty on the Functioning of the European Union, Article 35 states, “Quantitative
restrictions on exports, and all measures having equivalent effect, shall be
prohibited between Member States.” The PPE shortage of March 2020 illustrated
that even the European Union’s internal commitment was not sufficient to
prevent restrictions on trade between member states during an emergency. The
European Union’s response was to take control of the emergency situation; even
its export restrictions were temporary.
In the United States, negotiators have recently been considering additional
disciplines (or at least guidance) on export restrictions in several areas. First,
in the implementing regulations for the Inflation Reduction Act, the Treasury
Department included potential disciplines on export restrictions when it
established parameters for countries to sign critical minerals agreements with
the United States in order to qualify as a free trade agreement partner for the
purpose of battery electric vehicle tax credits under Section 30D. [70] Article 3(2)
of the US–Japan Critical Minerals Agreement of March 2023, for example, states
that “each Party shall maintain its current practice not to impose export duties on
critical minerals exported to the territory of the other Party.”
Second, the Indo-Pacific Economic Framework (IPEF) for Prosperity
Agreement Relating to Supply Chain Resilience, [71] Article 12(4) states that “in
the event of a supply chain disruption, the Parties intend to avoid unnecessary
actions that would exacerbate shortages and significantly impact IPEF supply
chains.” Numerous other elements of that agreement’s texts are about norms and
transparency designed to address potential shortages and work to limit the use
of export restrictions between IPEF member countries.
69 One explanation as to why export taxes were historically omitted from the GATT is that no
one needed to negotiate with the United States to constrain it from using them because of the
Constitutional constraint described earlier.
70 See Department of the Treasury, ‘Section 30D New Clean Vehicle Credit’, 88 Federal Register
23370, 17 April 2023.
71 The IPEF was a set of negotiations initiated by the Biden administration in 2022 that included
Australia, Brunei, Fiji, India, Indonesia, Japan, South Korea, Malaysia, New Zealand, the
Philippines, Singapore, Thailand, and Viet Nam.
WP 25-11 | MAY 2025 19
5. THINKING AHEAD
The failure of the trading system to sufficiently discipline the national use of
export restrictions has become problematic in an era of globalization in which
agglomeration economies lead to geographically concentrated production in
countries of concern for an increasing range of essential products. The ensuing
position of market dominance has resulted in forceful attempts by other
governments to break those agglomeration economies through the use of
industrial and trade policy.
However, these efforts to create additional sources of supply outside of
countries of concern impose economic costs on like-minded countries. Many
of the benefits to diversification are similar to those that arise when taking out
an insurance policy: The benefit pays off when a bad shock hits, but the costly
premium has to be paid even in good states of the world. Creation of additional
competition may prevent countries of concern from exercising their market
power, but it may also create efficiency losses, as competition through more
production locations may mean the realization of fewer scale economies and thus
higher production costs. The world would end up with fewer globally beneficial
examples of agglomeration economies, as economic security and the fear of
being cut off leads more countries to establish their own industries, all operating
at smaller scale.
Given these costs, even like-minded countries risk losing out if they do not
credibly commit not to use export restrictions against each other in the future,
for several reasons. First, failure to commit may cause them not to bother to
implement cooperative trade and industrial policies, increasing the likelihood
that they fail to address the economic forces that led to the initial agglomeration.
Second, even if they manage to create new sources of supply, doing so is
excessively costly, because it requires a more distorting use of industrial
policy and tariffs than was needed. Third, new sources of supply operating at
excessively higher cost may be difficult to sustain in light of continued efforts
by countries of concern to retain or even regain their market dominance. New
disciplines that limit the use of export restrictions between like-minded countries
are therefore a necessary part of the policy cooperation agenda needed to create
and sustain reasonably cost-effective alternative sources of supply outside of
countries of concern.

44 腾讯云出海服务解决方案
电商直播
业务场景
电商直播业务场景通常基于网络直播平台，实现跨国界的产品展示、互动营销和即时交易，因此需要保障主播与观众实时互动的流
畅性，并提供交易支持，以提升用户体验、促进销售转化。
解决方案
以腾讯云直播/视频SDK 为核心技术支持，结合弹幕IM、端对端方案、美颜特效、极速高清直播服务技术，支持海外直播答题、海外
直播卖货、弹幕打赏等电商直播互动场景。

2025年06月15日更新-【海通国际】2025年4月贸易数据点评：出口：三个超预期
——2025 年 4 月贸易数据点评
本报告导读：
4 月出口增速有三个超预期之处：一是对美出口增速韧性较强，或是受跨境电商抢
出口、豁免产品集中出口等因素影响；二是转口贸易几乎实现完全对冲，中间品出
口或简单再加工的影响截然不同；三是非转口地的贸易增速较高，说明中国出口商
可能正在积极寻找非美增量市场。后续出口或有一定回落，但总体韧性可期。
投资要点：
整体来看：
 同比增速上， 2025 年 4 月，美元计价的中国出口增速 8.1% （前值
12.4% ），进口增速 -0.2% （前值 -4.3% ）。
 从环比动能来看， 4 月出口较 3 月环比增速 0.6% ，与近年季节性基
本持平，考虑到 3 月可能的抢出口透支和 4 月关税落地，这一环比
数据呈现出极强的韧性；进口环比增速 3.9% ，强于季节性水平。
 商品贸易顺差强于 2025 年第一季度均值，对 4 月经济形成支撑。
出口结构上来看：
 国别结构上， 2025 年 4 月形成显著的三档分化：对美国出口显著
回落，录得 -21.0% ，对转口地东盟和拉美的出口显著提升，分别录得
20.8% 和 17.3% ，对其他地区的出口增速位于二者之前。
 商品结构上，尽管手机电脑被关税豁免，但出口增速仍然显著回落，
可能的原因是前期抢出口有所透支，关注 5 月 20 日对美具体产品
出口数据披露进一步验证。
4 月出口数据呈现出三重超预期之处：
 一是对美出口韧性超预期。 环比仅低于季节性 19 个百分点左右，出
口的关税弹性仅为 -0.17 。这一定程度上说明了美国进口商对中国出
口商品的高依赖度，此外可能的原因是：（ 1 ）关税仅针对 4 月 9 日
以后出港船舶加征，导致 4 月 1 日至 4 月 9 日延续着 3 月对美较强
的出口；（ 2 ）预计 5 月 2 日将正式实施的小包裹关税调整，引发了
4 月跨境电商一定程度的抢出口。
 二是转口贸易强度超预期。 2025 年 4 月中国对美国、东盟和拉美三
地合计的出口增速 4.7% ，说明转口贸易基本完全对冲了对美国出口
的减少。如果以简单再加工转口为主，则说明转口贸易不受东盟短
期产能限制影响；如果以中间品加工贸易为主，则可能意味着对后
续的出口和生产形成一定透支。有待 5 月 20 日公布的分国家分产
品的细分数据验证。
 三是对美国和转口地以外的国家地区出口超预期。 剔除美国、东盟
和拉美三地， 2025 年 4 月出口增速 10.4% ，强于 2024 年 Q4 均值
7.4% 和 2025 年 Q1 均值 4.5% ，说明中国出口商可能正在积极寻找非
美增量市场。
往后看，短期在可能的小包裹抢出口退温和转口产能受限的影响下，
出口或有一定退温，但 4 月呈现的积极表现一定程度证明了中国出
口的强大韧性。二季度内关税冲击下中国经济有望出现超 4 月市场
预期的表现。而中长期维度则需要观察中美贸易问题会谈的结果。
风险提示： 全球经济波动加大；美国关税策略调整。
出口：三个超预期
宏观专题
目录
出口环比呈现较强韧性 .................................................................................. 3
分国家看：转口地、非转口地、美国三档分化 ........................................... 4
分产品看：消费电子出口表现弱于预期 ....................................................... 4
短期出口超预期，中期关注中美贸易会谈 ................................................... 6
风险提示 .......................................................................................................... 7
宏观专题
1. 出口环比呈现较强韧性
2025 年 4 月，美元计价的中国出口增速 8.1% （前值 12.4% ），进口增速 -0.2%
（前值 -4.3% ）。 4 月商品贸易顺差维持高位，高于 2025 年第一季度均值。
从环比动能来看， 4 月出口较 3 月环比增速 0.6% ，与近年季节性基本持平，
考虑到 3 月可能的抢出口透支和 4 月关税落地，这一环比数据呈现出极强
的韧性；进口环比增速 3.9% ，强于季节性水平。
图 1 ： 4 月出口增速 8.1% ，进口同比 -0.2% 图 2 ： 4 月出口环比增速处于历史居中水平
数据来源： Wind ， HTI 数据来源： Wind ， HTI
图 3 ：出口总额超季节性回升 图 4 ：进口总额回升幅度基本处于季节性水平
数据来源： Wind ， HTI 数据来源： Wind ， HTI
宏观专题
2. 分国家看：转口地、非转口地、美国三档分化
出口方面， 2025 年 4 月形成显著的三档分化： 对美国出口显著回落，录得
-21.0% ，对转口地东盟和拉美的出口显著提升，分别录得 20.8% 和 17.3% ，
对其他地区的出口增速位于二者之前。
进口方面， 2025 年 4 月自美国和欧盟进口增速显著回落，自拉美进口显著
提升。可能的原因是对美关税反制背景下，自巴西进口大豆成为自美进口大
豆的替代品。
图 5 ：对东盟和拉美出口较强，对美出口回落（单位： % ） 图 6 ：自美欧进口回落，自拉美进口提升（单位： % ）
数据来源： Wind ， HTI 数据来源： Wind ， HTI
图 7 ：外需略有走弱 图 8 ：对美出口环比显著回落
数据来源： Wind ， HTI 数据来源： Wind ， HTI
3. 分产品看：消费电子出口表现弱于预期
出口方面，粮食、音视频设备、集成电路、船舶仍强。尽管手机电脑被关税
豁免，但出口增速仍然显著回落，可能的原因是前期抢出口有所透支，关注
5 月 20 日对美具体产品出口数据披露进一步验证。
进口方面，化工与部分贱金属产品回暖，集成电路、机床、电脑零部件在持
续 “ 抢进口 ” 。
宏观专题
图 9 ：出口：粮食、音视频设备、集成电路、船舶仍强（单位： % ）

宏观专题
4. 短期出口超预期，中期关注中美贸易会谈
4 月出口表现有三点超预期之处 ：
一是对美出口韧性超预期。 在 2024 年同期高基数的背景，和平均加征 110%
的超高关税税率下，中国对美出口增速回落幅度有限，环比仅低于季节性 19
个百分点左右，出口的关税弹性仅为 -0.17 。这一定程度上说明了美国进口
商对中国出口商品的高依赖度，此外可能的原因是：（ 1 ）关税仅针对 4 月 9
日以后出港船舶加征，导致 4 月 1 日至 4 月 9 日延续着 3 月对美较强的出
口；（ 2 ）预计 5 月 2 日将正式实施的小包裹关税调整，引发了 4 月跨境电
商一定程度的抢出口。
二是转口贸易强度超预期 。 2025 年 4 月中国对美国、东盟和拉美三地合计
的出口增速 4.7% ，其中对东盟、拉美两大转口地的出口增速分别为 20.8% 和
17.3% ，说明转口贸易基本完全对冲了对美国出口的减少。如果以简单再加
工转口为主，则说明转口贸易不受东盟短期产能限制影响；如果以中间品加
工贸易为主，则可能意味着对后续的出口和生产形成一定透支。有待 5 月
20 日公布的分国家分产品的细分数据验证。
三是对美国和转口地以外的国家地区出口超预期。 剔除美国、东盟和拉美
三地， 2025 年 4 月出口增速 10.4% ，强于 2024 年 Q4 均值 7.4% 和 2025 年
Q1 均值 4.5% ，说明中国出口商可能正在积极寻找非美增量市场。
往后看，短期在可能的小包裹抢出口退温和转口产能受限的影响下，出口或
有一定退温，但 4 月呈现的积极表现一定程度证明了中国出口的强大韧性。
二季度内关税冲击下中国经济有望出现超 4 月市场预期的表现。而中长期
维度则需要观察中美贸易问题会谈的结果。
图 11 ：考虑豁免，美国 2025 年对华平均加征了 110 个百分点关税
数据来源：白宫官网， USITC ， HTI
宏观专题
图 11 ： 2018-2019 年贸易摩擦导致转口趋势明显
数据来源： Wind ， HTI
5. 风险提示
全球经济波动加大；美国关税策略调整。

whom I have already notified of this, will not deal in or trade any securities in respect of the issuer that I review within 3 business days after the research report is published.

Outperform: The stock’s total return over the next 12-18 months is
expected to exceed the return of its relevant broad market benchmark, as
indicated below.
Neutral: The stock’s total return over the next 12-18 months is expected to
be in line with the return of its relevant broad market benchmark, as
indicated below. For purposes only of FINRA/NYSE ratings distribution
rules, our Neutral rating falls into a hold rating category.

expected to be below the return of its relevant broad market benchmark,

2
prices based on possible valuation multiples. Such rankings or suggested prices do not purport to be stock ratings or target prices or fundamental values and are for information only.
海通国际 A 股覆盖： 海通国际可能会就沪港通及深港通的中国 A 股进行覆盖及评级。国泰海通证券（ 601211.CH ），海通国际于上海的母公司，也会于中国发布中国 A 股的研究报告。
但是，海通国际使用与国泰海通证券不同的评级系统，所以海通国际与国泰海通证券的中国 A 股评级可能有所不同。
Haitong International Coverage of A-Shares: Haitong International may cover and rate A-Shares that are subject to the Hong Kong Stock Connect scheme with Shanghai and Shenzhen. GUOTAI
HAITONG SECURITIES (601211 CH), the ultimate parent company of HTISG based in Shanghai, covers and publishes research on these same A-Shares for distribution in mainland China. However, the
rating system employed by GTHS differs from that used by HTI and as a result there may be a difference in the HTI and GTHS ratings for the same A-share stocks.
海通国际优质 100 A 股（ Q100 ）指数： 海通国际 Q100 指数是一个包括 100 支由国泰海通证券覆盖的优质中国 A 股的计量产品。这些股票是通过基于质量的筛选过程，并结合对国泰海
通证券 A 股团队自下而上的研究。海通国际每季对 Q100 指数成分作出复审。
Haitong International Quality 100 A-share (Q100) Index: HTI’s Q100 Index is a quant product that consists of 100 of the highest-quality A-shares under coverage at GTHS in Shanghai. These stocks
are carefully selected through a quality-based screening process in combination with a review of the GTHS A-share team’s bottom-up research. The Q100 constituent companies are reviewed
quarterly.

第一条 义利（ FIN-ESG ）数据系由盟浪可持续数字科技有限责任公司（以下简称 “ 本公司 ” ）基于合法取得的公开信息评估而成，本公司对信息的准确性及完整性不作任何保证。对公司
的评估结果仅供参考，并不构成对任何个人或机构投资建议，也不能作为任何个人或机构购买、出售或持有相关金融产品的依据。本公司不对任何个人或机构投资者因使用本数据表
述的评估结果造成的任何直接或间接损失负责。
第二条 盟浪并不因收到此评估数据而将收件人视为客户，收件人使用此数据时应根据自身实际情况作出自我独立判断。本数据所载内容反映的是盟浪在最初发布本数据日期当日的判
断，盟浪有权在不发出通知的情况下更新、修订与发出其他与本数据所载内容不一致或有不同结论的数据。除非另行说明，本数据（如财务业绩数据等）仅代表过往表现，过往的业
绩表现不作为日后回报的预测。
第三条 本数据版权归本公司所有，本公司依法保留各项权利。未经本公司事先书面许可授权，任何个人或机构不得将本数据中的评估结果用于任何营利性目的，不得对本数据进行修
改、复制、编译、汇编、再次编辑、改编、删减、缩写、节选、发行、出租、展览、表演、放映、广播、信息网络传播、摄制、增加图标及说明等，否则因此给盟浪或其他第三方造

公文包

2025年06月20日更新海尔智家(600690)首次覆盖报告：高端出海齐头并进，世界海尔扬帆起航-250619-西部证券-28页
高端出海齐头并进，世界海尔扬帆起航
海尔智家（ 600690.SH ）首次覆盖报告
 核心结论
行业供需前景稳定，海尔冰洗基本盘�位稳�， 1 ）家电内销进入成熟期，
更新换代成为主要需求，�内白电龙头成本规模优势明显，长期驱动行业结
构升级； 2 ）海尔冰洗基本盘长期份额领先，品牌制造能力强大，全球生产
研发体系布局完善，竞争优势长期维系； 3 ）公司数字化建设有效赋能，运
营提效 & 产品推新下，消费者竞争实力进一步增强。
高端化前瞻布局进入收获期，卡萨帝表现亮眼， 1 ）卡萨帝品牌通过深入洞
察本�消费者高端细分需求，品牌业绩持续高增，成功斩获用户心智，带动
公司升级； 2 ）�超高端价格带卡萨帝已顺利实现份额占领，未来销量仍有
充足空间，伴随高净值人群稳步扩容，有望实现良好发展。
自有品牌出海进程领先，潜�增量空间广阔， 1 ）海尔出海进程领先、体系
完备，品牌、制造本�化程度高，且成功建设全球研发、制造、供应链体系，
实现有效协同赋能，海外布局具备一定稀缺性。 2 ）海尔已是全球大家电领
军者，但市占率情况分�区分品类拆分来看，仍存�较大提升空间，考虑到
海尔全球竞争优势以及自身积极布局，未来有望持续转化份额，单看优势冰
洗业务，增量空间也十分可观，其他品类潜�空间更大，且公司有望充分承
接新兴市�人口 & 经济红利，远期空间广阔。
投资建议： 海尔作为全球家电品类引领者，品牌制造 / 创新研发多环节领先，
份额优势有望长期保持；此外公司眼光长远前瞻布局，多年持续培育产品高
端化与品类出海，目前已进入收获期，高端价格带份额领先、海外新兴市�
也持续高增贡献，产业�位不断加强；未来数字化变革进一步深入、海外本
布局逐步完善、以及短期�补拉动等叠素，海尔业绩有望持续增长。预计
25-27 年归母净利润 211/232/254 亿，对应 pe11.0/10.0/9.1X ，长期配置价
值凸显，看好海尔全球竞争优势，首次覆盖给予“买入”评级。
风险提示：生产安全问题、原材料价格波动、消费力恢复不及预期、竞争加
剧超预期、贸易形势变化超预期、汇率变动风险。
 核心数据

4 | 请务必仔细阅读报告尾部的投资评级说明和声明
公司深度研究 | 海尔智家
投资要点
关键假设
西部证券
2025 年 06 月 19 日
1 、国内白电行业长期结构升级拉动，供需两侧前景稳固，海尔品牌 / 产品结构位居行业前
列，份额优势有望长期保持，叠加国补短期正向贡献，预计 25/26/27 内销收入同比
+8%/5%/5% 。
2 、海尔海外布局完善，掌握核心品牌资源、多区域市占率领先，新兴市场有望双位数以
上持续高增，带动外销整体增长，预计 25/26/27 外销收入同比 +7%/6%/6% 。
3 、分品类看，海尔冰洗竞争优势明确，未来有望保持稳健发展，预计 25-27 冰洗收入同
比 +7%/+5%/+5% ；空调产业伴随公司投入力度加大以及产能补齐，产业地位有望进一步
加强，预计 25-27 同比 +11%/+8%/+7% 。
4 、此外公司郑大力建设数字化体系，在产品侧、自身运营侧均存在有效赋能，自身灵活
度进一步增强，有望持续带动费用率优化、盈利水平提升，预计公司 25/26/27 归母净利
率水平分别 6.9%/7.2%/7.4% 。
区别于市场的观点
市场对国内白电行业成长性、以及海外市场需求有所分歧。我们认为白电内销长期结构升
级拉动，海外可拓展空间充足，产业长期稳健增长无虞。对于海尔而言，公司已有完善的
全球体系布局、以及完备的产品矩阵，同时公司对高端化、品牌、数字化提效等环节的投
入进入收获期，自身综合竞争实力有望进一步加强兑现。
股价上涨催化剂
冰箱 / 洗衣机 / 空调等行业短期销量数据，尤其海尔自身份额情况，以及高端品牌卡萨帝零
售端情况，均为重要短期催化；此外公司出海布局领先，海外成熟市场需求改善，以及对
新兴市场的持续挖掘，均会对股价所有提振；除此之外，公司大力推动数字化转型，带来
的产业链效率提升，与自身费用优化，同样是重要催化。
投资建议
海尔作为全球家电品类引领者，品牌制造 / 创新研发多环节领先，份额优势有望长期保持；
此外公司眼光长远前瞻布局，多年持续培育产品高端化与品类出海，目前已进入收获期，
高端价格带份额领先、海外新兴市场也持续高增贡献，产业地位不断加强；未来数字化变
革进一步深入、海外本土布局逐步完善、以及短期国补拉动等因素，海尔业绩有望持续增
长。预计 25-27 年归母净利润 211/232/254 亿，对应 pe11.0/10.0/9.1X ，长期配置价值凸
显，看好海尔全球竞争优势，首次覆盖给予“买入”评级。

公司深度研究 | 海尔智家
一、行业供需前景稳定，海尔基本盘地位稳固
西部证券
2025 年 06 月 19 日
1.1 行业：内销进入成熟期，供需前景稳固，长期结构升级驱动
家电内销进入成熟期。 产业在线销量口径看，伴随国内整体增长中枢放缓，冰空洗等白电
内销进入成熟期，但对应市场规模十分巨大，同时行业竞争格局长期稳定、结构稳步升级，
在位龙头规模 / 品牌侧长期存在优势，未来仍将获得稳健机遇；此外由于中国家电全球竞争
优势，产业外销比例不断提升，逐步贡献增量。
图 2 ：洗衣机内 / 外销量（万台） 图 3 ：空调内 / 外销量（万台）
资料来源：产业在线、西部证券研发中心 资料来源：产业在线、西部证券研发中心
图 4 ：冰箱内 / 外销量（万台） 图 5 ：油烟机内 / 外销量（万台）
资料来源：产业在线、西部证券研发中心 资料来源：产业在线、西部证券研发中心
更新换代成为行业主导，需求结构稳定。 1 ）渗透率角度看，冰洗由于“一户一台”属性，
保有量接近饱和； 2 ）品类需求拆分看，在渗透率饱和、外部投资活动放缓背景下，更新
换代逐渐占主导地位，冰洗绝大部分销量由以旧换新推动，与地产链相关系数有所下降，
行业需求结构更加稳定，更易于龙头产业升级。
7 | 请务必仔细阅读报告尾部的投资评级说明和声明
公司深度研究 | 海尔智家
图 6 ：冰箱 / 空调 / 洗衣机城镇居民每百户保有量（台） 图 7 ：白电需求结构拆分
西部证券
2025 年 06 月 19 日
供给侧长期约束，品牌更易长期积累。 1 ）白电制造属性看规模优势，白电龙头企业规模
暨成本优势强； 2 ）稳定的供给格局，叠加白电用消费品属性，品牌迭代十分缓慢，更新
周期远高于快消品，用户心智长久积累，品牌更易沉淀，后来者难以居上。
图 8 ：空调为例，中国产能全球占比高
资料来源：产业在线、西部证券研发中心
品质之外功能诉求凸显，行业结构长期升级。 1 ）以冰洗为例，耐用性 & 基础功能之外，
功能性诉求不断提升，消费者越买越好，龙头需不断通过创新迭代升级； 2 ）长周期产品
结构上看，行业高端机型占比不断提升，观念 / 技术共同驱动结构稳步升级。

1.2 格局：海尔冰洗基本盘地位稳固，份额 / 结构领先
冰洗海尔优势区，份额优势长期稳固。 公司以冰洗起家，沉淀深厚，通过品牌力建设及技
术积累，公司冰洗市占率长期稳居龙头，份额优势显著。其中，洗衣机与美的形成双寡头
格局，冰箱品类市占率第一，且长期来看公司冰洗份额稳中有升，龙头地位稳固。

誉度良好，自身产品结构中大容量高端机型占比更大，叠加自身品牌力加持，公司均价、

1.3 公司优势：品牌制造能力强大，数字化体系建设有效赋能
强大的品牌制造能力是基础，海尔体系布局完善。 过往持续深耕以及外延并购整合，海尔
在全球范围内已率先形成完备的生产制造 & 研发体系布局，不同区域品牌相互赋能，此外
公司重视创新投入，研发费用率对比看，处于较高一档。

二、高端化表现亮眼，出海空间仍然广阔
2.1 高端化：前瞻布局卡萨帝，高端价格带份额领先
西部证券
2025 年 06 月 19 日
卡萨帝高端价格带份额领先，持续高增。 海尔领先行业布局高端化，卡萨帝品牌长久打磨
积淀深厚，目前进入收获期，过往期间持续高增，对公司形成有效拉动，份额情况上看，
卡萨帝高端价格带地位稳固，尤其万元以上市场独占鳌头。

竞争格局良好，引领行业高端扩容。 历史上国内高端家电曾是外资天下，卡萨帝凭借更深
的本土需求洞察，以及更强的产品 / 渠道能力成功完成追赶，消费者美誉度 / 品牌势能等方
面均位居行业前列 ，同时引领行业高端市场不断扩容。

卡萨帝擅长挖掘高端细分需求，产品不断创新迭代积累用户心智。 1 ）卡萨帝善于发现用
户需求痛点，依托海尔全球范围技术沉淀，卡萨帝从产品设计、基础技术创新等环节做突
破，以往成功通过智能分区、家电平嵌等创新概念打造差异化卖点，同时公司持续进行产
品迭代，不断打磨创新能力、优化产品体验，成功沉淀消费者信任； 2 ）目前卡萨帝在多
个高端家电品类中产品力均位居前列，成为国内高端家电代名词。

核心卖点/解决痛点

说明

单区-> 分
区

冰箱

传统冰箱串味、食材腐坏变
质快，卡萨帝提出营养分区、
吃的精制。

•技术：全球首创MSA 控氧保鲜技术，通过降低储鲜空间氧含量，使食材储存天数领
先行业；
•产品不断迭代：5 个多功能制冷室的法式六门冰箱-> F+系列储鲜冰箱->首创母婴
专属空间的婴爱冰箱->24 年「高分智感」系列，有蔬果、立式设计饮品独立专区

单区-> 分
区

冰吧

卡萨帝针对传统冰吧无法分
门别类储存物品进行改进

•打造典藏酒区、多维冷藏区、变温区, 茶叶、护肤品、乳品都有更专业的精储空间。

单区-> 分
区

**洗衣机 **

传统洗衣机混洗，不能保证
所有面料都洗得净,也容易

•技术：原创双子星智平衡系统和钢骨一体结构，整机重量更轻，和竞品比运行更
平稳；
分区概念不断迭代：衣物分区->中子和美洗干护理机->中子融朗洗烘护一体机，

单区-> 分
区

空调

吹空调时,老人、小孩和成人
适合的温度不同，卡萨帝双
出风口设计。

•首创双塔设计,搭载两个独立风口,针对不同人群打造专属舒适软风。•产品迭代：之
后推出主动软风的鉴赏家空调、实现双塔独立精控的星云空调、24 年推出致境空调，
实现可变软风，让不同场景、不同需求的人群都能享受舒适温度。

嵌入-> 原
创平嵌

平嵌冰箱

传统冰箱橱柜风格很难相
融,有闪缝、凸出等问题；卡
萨帝率先提出自由嵌入概
念,将散热缝隙从10cm 缩至
2cm。

•技术：原创双轴变轨铰链,可以大角度开启冰箱门，二是将冰箱的制冷系统全部“转
移”到底部，冰箱正面可以平嵌安装，两侧实现“零嵌”；
•近百人技术团队，历经5 年、2 万小时、20 万次的反复实验才成功实现。

嵌入-> 原
创平嵌

平嵌洗衣
机

传统洗衣机嵌入橱柜后凸
出、浪费空间,在视觉和使用
感上都大打折扣，卡萨帝进
行改进。

•双子洗衣机实现了5mm 极限嵌装,同时以直角线条、无把手筒门实现纯平嵌入橱柜。
资料来源：海尔智家公司公告、卡萨帝官网、西部证券研发中心
品牌调性长期打造，品牌价值美誉度良好。 1 ）卡萨帝通过圈层营销持续打造高端品牌形
象，在品宣上偏好高端媒体与峰会合作，并在主流高端商场举办艺术巡展，强化品牌艺术
性与大气格局，同时引导用户体验高品质生活方式 ； 2 ）此外提供全流程高品质服务，其
售后内容与覆盖范围远超行业常规标准如更长的保修期、免费洗护等，以差异化体验强化
消费者品牌认同。

2.2 高端化：卡萨帝渗透率空间充足，看好高端稳步扩容
套系化成为高端家电重要驱动，三翼鸟加持下卡萨帝势能有望提升。 套系销售节省消费者
比较成本，尤其高端家电，原先卡萨帝已在家电连锁、高端商场、海尔品牌店及线上主流
平台完成全面渠道布局，近年海尔重点发力三翼鸟品牌，门店内全面导入卡萨帝产品，旨
在通过场景化情景展现，实现从产品向高端生活方式转型，门店销售持续高增，为卡萨帝
品牌发展注入新动力，此外套系销售也助力于卡萨帝品类横向拓展。
表 9 ：海尔渠道层级下沉与扁平化情况简述


时间

成效

套系产品销量+41%，高端智慧成套销量+63.2%

2023

2024

2.3 出海：掌握核心品牌资源，全球化体系有效赋能协同
海尔自有品牌外销进程领先，海外布局稀缺性。 海尔自主品牌出海、外延收购并行，海外
白电有较强区域保护属性，消费者品牌忠诚度也更强，优质品牌资源更是有限，尤其成熟
市场；海尔过去成功整合 GE 、 Fisher&paykel 等多个优质标的，品牌 / 技术 / 渠道等环节相
互协同赋能，海外布局具备稀缺性。

2.4 出海：未来空间依旧广阔，新兴市场贡献增量
分品类、分地区海尔份额看： 东南亚、亚非拉等新兴市场家电行业仍处在发展阶段，竞争
格局尚未成熟、人口红利大、家电保有率有提升空间，且海尔在新兴市场已有一定市场根
基，随着行业规模的发展和空调份额的补齐，新兴市场有望成为未来主要增长动力。

三、未来看点
3.1 国补形成有效拉动，高端更加受益
短期国补形成有效拉动，高端结构更加受益。 1 ） 24 年国补对行业需求提振已得到显著验
证，短期补贴落地相当于变相增加消费者购买力，产品结构较高品种更加受益。 2 ） 24 年
表 16 ：政策补贴后家电景气度提升 图 54 ：卡萨帝洗衣机线上销售额增速国补后好于行业

3.2 高端化拉动，叠加数字化降本增效，利润弹性可期
降本增效 / 结构拉动作用下，盈利水平有望提升。 1 ）公司正大力推进数字化改革，研发 /
流通 / 制造 / 营销多环节效率提升， 25 年改革进入深水区，期间费用率有望持续优化； 2 ）
海尔自身冰洗基本盘稳固，份额稳步提升，此外空调压缩机等核心零部件自主产能补齐，
公司整体竞争实力进一步强化，带动盈利水平提升。

3.3 海外组织提效、高端化并行，新兴市场持续高增
西部证券
2025 年 06 月 19 日
海外扩张同时讲究运营质量，量价空间进一步打开。 1 ）公司海外持续做优质品牌收购、
产能渠道扩张等动作，同时注重产品创新与品类推展，尤其高端系列； 2 ）伴随海外数字
化变革逐渐落地，组织进一步精简提效，以及新兴市场持续放量下的规模效应增强，利润
率弹性可期。
表 17 ：海尔全球化体系介绍


Col1

说明



•收购开利商用制冷业务、南非热水器龙头Kwikot

渠道建设

•新兴市场渠道扩张，印度等区域线上线下渠道网络铺设渠道覆盖率提升，
基本完成全国覆盖
•沙特等地渠道变革，致力提升区域代理积极性
•欧美成熟市场深化高端零售、工程等渠道

品类扩张、高端化


•如印度市场差异高端组合(高能效空调/大容量洗衣机等)
•25Q1 北美高端品牌收入双位数增长、厨电品类扩张

组织提效、供应链
优化

•24 年关闭欧洲低效工厂，精简组织提效
•加强海外供应链运营升级，越南、印尼等基地智能化制造升级

海外数字化变革

•海外复制国内数字化模式，泰国、中东非建立客户体验平台，加强线上投
入；多家海外工厂落地数字化系统，效率提升

四、盈利预测与投资评级
核心假设：
西部证券
2025 年 06 月 19 日
1 、国内白电行业长期结构升级拉动，供需两侧前景稳固，海尔品牌 / 产品结构位居行业前
列，份额优势有望长期保持，叠加国补短期正向贡献，预计 25/26/27 内销收入同比
+8%/5%/5% 。
2 、海尔海外布局完善，掌握核心品牌资源、多区域市占率领先，新兴市场有望双位数以
上持续高增，带动外销整体增长，预计 25/26/27 外销收入同比 +7%/6%/6% 。
加强，预计 25-27 同比 +11%/+8%/+7% 。
4 、此外公司郑大力建设数字化体系，在产品侧、自身运营侧均存在有效赋能，自身灵活
度进一步增强，有望持续带动费用率优化、盈利水平提升，预计公司 25/26/27 归母净利
率水平分别 6.9%/7.2%/7.4% 。

投资建议：
海尔作为全球家电品类引领者，品牌制造 / 创新研发多环节领先，份额优势有望长期保持；
此外公司眼光长远前瞻布局，多年持续培育产品高端化与品类出海，目前已进入收获期，
高端价格带份额领先、海外新兴市场也持续高增贡献，产业地位不断加强；未来数字化变
革进一步深入、海外本土布局逐步完善、以及短期国补拉动等因素，海尔业绩有望持续增
长。预计 25-27 年归母净利润 211/232/254 亿，对应 pe11.0/10.0/9.1X ，长期配置价值凸
显，看好海尔全球竞争优势，首次覆盖给予“买入”评级。

注： 1) 可比公司性公司选取方法：海尔是国内“三大白”之一，所以国内公司选取格力美的对比，同时海外布局行业领先，
五、风险提示
生产安全问题：制造、运输、安装、维修环节若发生安全问题，损害企业声誉。
原材料价格波动：若原材料价格上行较大，将降低企业盈利水平。
消费力恢复不及预期：若宏观经济、消费力疲软，将导致行业销量不及预期。
竞争加剧超预期：行业价格竞争进一步加剧，将影响企业盈利能力。
贸易形势变化超预期：关税频繁变化、包括外部进一步出台限制我国外贸的其他政策，影
响家电出口，对国内价盘形成挤压。
汇率变动风险：公司海外业务占比较高，若汇率大幅度波动，会对公司收益产生影响。

2025 年 06 月 19 日


行业评级

超配： 行业预期未来6-12 个月内的涨幅超过市场基准指数10%以上
中配： 行业预期未来6-12 个月内的波动幅度介于市场基准指数-10%到10%之间
低配： 行业预期未来6-12 个月内的跌幅超过市场基准指数10%以上


公司评级


买入： 公司未来6-12 个月的投资收益率领先市场基准指数20%以上
增持： 公司未来6-12 个月的投资收益率领先市场基准指数5%到20%之间
中性： 公司未来6-12 个月的投资收益率与市场基准指数变动幅度相差-5%到5%
卖出： 公司未来6-12 个月的投资收益率落后市场基准指数大于5%
报告中所涉及的投资评级采用相对评级体系，基于报告发布日后 6-12 个月内公司股价（或行业指数）相对同期当地市场基准指数的市
场表现预期。其中， A 股市场以沪深 300 指数为基准；香港市场以恒生指数为基准；美国市场以标普 500 指数为基准。
的信息，独立、客观地出具本报告。本报告清晰准确地反映了本人的研究观点。本人不曾因，不因，也将不会因本报告中的具体推荐意
见或观点而直接或间接收到任何形式的补偿。

2025年07月04日更新-【同程商旅】2025年中国企业出海差旅研究报告

同程商旅《2025中国企业出海差旅研究报告》联合伙伴
研究方法与数据来源
本报告的研究基于多元数据来源与综合分析方法，确保结论的洞察性与前瞻性。
 同程商旅自有平台数据
基于多年积累的企业差旅管理数据，
提供了全面的市场洞察
 GBTA（全球商旅协会）数据
结合GBTA发布的最新行业报告，为全
球及中国商旅管理趋势提供了参考
 网络公开信息整理
结合行业报告、企业案例和公开数据，
丰富分析视角
 问卷调研
面向不同行业的差旅负责人与商旅人士，
收集企业差旅需求与管理现状
 在线调研与座谈
通过在线调研与座谈，深入了解企业
差旅管理中的挑战与需求
 深度访谈
与行业专家、商旅平台负责人及大企业差旅管理
高管进行深度访谈，获取行业趋势的独到见解
出海经济 | 从产业到市场共振，出海转向战略必选项
近年来我国众多企业积极拓展海外市场，布局全球业务，展现出强劲的国际化发展趋势。出海从“可选项”转为“必选项”，呈现全产业、多维度、深
层次的综合输出模式，驱动着全球价值链重构。
（万亿元）

2024年出海规模达2.17万亿美元，占GDP 12.7%， 同比增长18.3%
全行业对外直接投资11592.7亿元人民币，同比增长11.3%
2025年出海规模预计突破2.5万亿美元
从传统贸易转向 “技术+品牌+标准” 综合输出
更多采用“产品+服务+数据”复合模式，技术许可收入增速显著
东南亚、拉美、中东等地区的占比有所上升，美国占比有所收缩
东南亚中企 市占率超40% ，电商、新能源主导
中东成为数字经济与绿色科技新热土
涵盖电子商务、信息技术、医疗健康、先进制造等众多领域
科创型企业 成为引领国际化新浪潮的重要力量
促进外贸稳定增长的若干政策
优化口岸营商环境的十六条措施
中小企业出海 服务专项行动、扩大出口信用保险覆盖面等……

商旅大盘 | 中国商旅市场韧性凸显，国际差旅需求强劲
GBTA数据显示，2023年中国商旅市场总规模达3,725亿美元，2024年预估达4,062亿美元，全面恢复赶超2019年水平，预计未来几年中国商旅市场将继
续保持稳定增长。
2024年中国企业跨境差旅持续恢复，国际出行的差旅需求占企业总需求比重已提升至3.3%，2025年上半年占比已达到4.9%，预计仍将持续提升。
（百亿美元）

差旅预算 | 预算策略理性平衡，海外扩张驱动预算上浮
企业差旅预算调研显示，57.9%企业将在2025年维持预算基本不变，24%企业计划上浮，其中计划海外预算上浮的企业有33.2%，较国内多9个百分点，
上浮30%以上的企业占比6.1%。
行业上看，信息互联网、旅游出行等外向型行业扩张显著，医疗、能源化工增速次之，部分行业反向收缩，折射经济全球化不确定局势下的理性平衡。

0%
扩大自贸协定网络： 目前签署自贸协定增至22个，2022年1月1日区域全面经济伙伴关
系 协定(RCEP)正式生效实施；推进加入CPTPP（全面与进步跨太平洋伙伴关系协定）
和DEPA（数字经济伙伴关系协定），深化区域经贸合作。
高质量共建“一带一路”： 十年累计贸易19.1万亿美元，实施中老铁路等重大项目及
“小而美”民生工程。
稳外贸政策加码 ：2023年国务院印发外贸稳规模优结构意见，各省配套措施支持企业
出海。
人民币国际化加速： 金砖国家筹建本币清算平台，支持跨境贸易结算，减少美元依赖。
稳外资专项行动： 《2025年稳外资行动方案》鼓励外资股权投资，优化服务保障与投
资促进。
加强出境游： 同程旅行顺应出入境经济发展，推动业务模式创新，加强出境游供应链，
国际业务收入显著增长，发布《2025中国高消费旅客出境游洞察》，聚焦白金会员偏
好，指导国际产品分层运营。
全球经济重心转移： 新兴经济体崛起，亚洲、非洲等地区经济增长迅速，消费市场
潜力巨大，为企业出海提供广阔空间，可降低对传统欧美市场的依赖。
新兴市场消费崛起 ：东南亚、印度等新兴市场中产阶级规模扩大，消费需求升级，
对品质产品和服务需求增加，企业出海面临新市场机遇。
全球产业链重构： 贸易保护主义推动全球产业链调整，部分产业向东南亚等地区转
移，企业可借此机遇拓展海外生产基地，优化供应链布局。
全球数字化浪潮 ：互联网普及和数字技术发展，跨境电商、数字服务等新兴业态兴
起，企业能突破地域限制，以数字化方式触达全球客户。
全球绿色转型： 全球对环保和可持续发展重视，绿色产业成为新风口，企业可凭借
绿色技术与产品出海，开拓海外市场，提升国际竞争力。
自由贸易协定增多： 全球自由贸易协定数量增加，区域经济一体化加深，企业享受
更低关税、更便利贸易条件，降低出海成本，提高市场准入便利性。
出海趋势 | 出海频率持续提升，属地化运营布局深化
2024年单个企业的国际差旅平均为322人次，增长率较2023年峰值90.1%略下降6.1个百分点，2025年人次持续增加，预计增速放缓至46%。
近年来中国企业出海差旅结构逐步变化，2024年出境差旅占比较上一年小幅回落，2025上半年有所提升；纯境外差旅当前占比已超40%，反映出企业全
球化进程中的属地化运营布局深化，商旅服务重心也同步转向属地资源整合。
（人次）

热门目的地 | 区域分布差异化，行业出海呈现新格局
跨境差旅目的地区中，东南亚热度相对较高，区域经济一体化及产业链转移驱动商旅活跃，北美地区受地缘政策抑制增速，中东依托能源转型及“数字
丝路”成新增长极，反映“一带一路”深化布局。
在各行业的差旅目的地集中度上，金融服务业更多去往欧洲地区，北美是教育科研行业热土，而东南亚则是批发零售行业的出海首选目的地。

热门目的地 | 亚太北美国家成热门，短途航线助力商旅复苏
2024年国际差旅各国频次上，印度尼西亚热度居首，其次是马来西亚、美国、日本等，整体呈现以亚太和北美为主的差旅集中趋势，反映区域经济合作
和产业联动的活跃度差异。
城市上看，雅加达、首尔、曼谷等地排名前列，航班恢复及短途航线价格理性化推动高频商旅。

热门目的地 | 东南亚各地热度波动，印尼雅加达领跑
作为中国企业出海差旅热度最高的区域，东南亚各国家和城市表现各有差异。国家上，印尼差旅占比逐年提升，2025上半年达23%，与越南、泰国持
续两年占据前三；城市上，排名首位的雅加达在2025年呈现大幅提升趋势，槟城热度稍低但上半年增势显著，曼谷则略有降温。
雅加达 曼谷 吉隆坡 新加坡 河内 胡志明 海防 槟城
2024年 2025年H1
出行趋势 | 差旅热度全年上扬，联程出行占比提升
整体上看，2024年国际差旅热度随着月份持续增加，除2月春节假期影响外，7月、9月、11月小幅回落，出海热度在12月达到年度峰值。
差旅天数上，广告文娱行业周期最长，近3.5天，其次是农林牧渔、批发零售等达2.5天以上；多城市联程差旅近4年来占比逐步提升，2025上半年已超
20%，中国企业出海的全球网格化布局渐趋成熟。

近期形势 | 国际国内多重变量交织，出海不确定性加剧
破浪出海 | 出海阶段需求分化，韧性迎变&合规安全成焦点
行业观点 | 出海领航：全球经贸变局下的战略破局之道
2025年【出海领航】发布《百家中国制造企业出海调查》，直击企业出海核心痛点，探讨全球化新格局下的市场扩张风险与机遇。
中国企业国际化拓展中面临的主要痛点：
风险多、融入难、看不清、管不住、韧性差
风险多:各地政策监管及法规复杂多样，
合规性要求高
融入难:文化环境差异及沟通障碍，严重
影响本地融入和运营效率

行业观点 | 出海领航：全球经贸变局下的战略破局之道
【出海领航】2025年《百家中国制造企业出海调查》数据显示，79%的受访企业认为公司未来海外业务占比会超过30%。.着眼于更长远的未来，调研企业充分展现了
中国出海企业对于挺进海外市场的坚定信念。
多数企业对于 未来3-5年 的海外业务发展充满信心
海外业务高速增长，占比接近或超过50%
海外业务稳步发展，海外业务占比接近30%
有选择的发展海外业务，海外业务占比接近10%
公司对海外业务的认识和投入都不充分，近期不考虑开发海外业务
其他
*数据来源：出海领航2025百家中国制造企业出海调查报告
破浪出海 | 出海解决方案模型，六维能力助破局

出海领航 | 行业差异化差旅策略，跨境场景快速适配
同时，各行业企业存在差异化出海需求，跨境商旅出行因此场景各异，企业需结合行业特性定制策略，通过专业平台降本增效，精准匹配跨境场景需求。

痛点洞察 | 成本与安全双忧，员工跨境多重关切
调研结果显示，海外差旅管理中，超60%差旅负责人最关心协议资源成本优势，其次是海外服务本土化能力和员工海外安全保障问题；52.1%的员工最
关心差旅标准与海外物价匹配度，近半数员工考量当地安全与紧急联络机制。

管理现状 | 多渠道预订模式并存，TMC管控带来多重效益
调研数据显示，中国企业国际差旅中有30%以上通过OTA平台完成预订，其次是企业与航司酒店签订的协议资源以及TMC（专业差旅管理机构），各
占25%左右，TMC在国际差旅管理方面的普及率仍有较大提升空间。
对于已通过TMC进行国际差旅管理的企业而言，近80%管理者认为可降低出海成本，70%以上认为提高全球统一管控效率且保障了海外财税的合规性。
全球优质资源，降低出海成本
统一渠道，全球差旅高效管控
结算模式适配，确保海外合规
规避员工海外出行的安全风险
7×24小时全球应急服务支持
全面提升员工的国际差旅体验

成本管控 | 人均成本回落渐平稳，偏好结构新演化
国际差旅单次人均成本上，2022年达到峰值后逐渐回落，2024及2025上半年基本保持在4,200元左右，反映近年来企业收紧的差旅政策渐趋稳定。
国际航班舱位选择上，超值经济舱青睐值逐渐降低，其余舱位均持续提升；2025上半年，五星和三星级国际酒店占比减少，四星级酒店首次超过50%，
凭借其入住体验、较高性价比以及安全合规性成为企业出海员工的下榻首选。

成本管控 | 国际机票均价回升，行业级价差持续扩大
2024年国际机票价格整体持续走低，主因中美增班、东南亚航线供给放量；2025上半年迎来约4.6个百分点的小幅回升，但仍低于2019年水平，航空运
力恢复与商务需求持续博弈。受RCEP区域航空服务自由化条款的落实以及地缘政治和关税贸易战等影响，2025下半年价格仍存波动可能。
行业层面，2024年运输仓储行业均价大幅提高，其他行业均小幅波动；2025上半年以批发零售、信息互联网等行业为首，预订均价大幅提升。
